## Preface

Patrol officers are the most frequent type of police officer. In nearly all departments, they constitute roughly two-thirds of the sworn personnel.1 Patrol officers also “provide almost all emergency help; their patrolling is probably the most effective crime prevention measure; and they are the only police . . . most people will ever meet.”2 Consequently, the quality of contemporary policing as well as its future quality are largely in the hands of patrol officers. Until very recently not very much was known about the behavior of police patrol officers. Sociological research had been infrequent3 and of the investigations undertaken, most had been devoted almost exclusively to such specialized police divisions as detective and traffic bureaus.4 However, personnel in specialized divisions account for less than a quarter of sworn police officers.5 Fortunately, there has been a recent acceleration in research on police, and this research has taken on a broader orientation. 1 See Terry Eisenberg, Deborah Ann Kent, and Charles R. W all, Police Personnel Practices in State and Local Governments. Washington, D.C': Police Foundation, 1973, p. 33. 2 David H . Bayley, Forces of Order: Police Behavior in Japan and the United States. Berkeley, Calif.: University of California Press, 1976, p. x. 3 See David H. Bayley, “The Police and Political Change in Comparative Perspective,” Law and Society Review 6(1971): 19-112. 4 Jerome Skolnick’s excellent work, Justice Without Trial, is an example as Skolnick tended to ignore routine patrol work in favor of specialized divisions such as vice and traffic. See Jerome Skolnick, Justice Without Trial. N ew York: John W iley and Sons, Inc., 1966. 5 Eisenberg, et al., Police Personnel Practices, p. 33.

## V iii / PREFACE

For instance, sociologists Albert J. Reiss, Jr. and Donald Black placed a total of 36 observers with police patrol officers in Boston, Washington, D.C., and Chicago.6 Historian and sociologist Jonathan Rubinstein entered the Philadelphia Police Academy and subsequently spent nearly two years observing patrol officers.7 And sociologists Richard E. Sykes and John P. Clark placed eight observers with police patrol officers in a large midwestern city.8 As a consequence of these researches as well as others9 we now know a great deal more about the nature of police patrol behavior. We possess a much better understanding of the types of citizen-initiated problems that police patrol officers confront.10 We also have a much better understanding of the social interaction that occurs during police-citizen encounters.11 In 6 For a description and summary of this research project see Albert J. Reiss, Jr., The Police and the Public. ## PREFACE / ix

addition, our descriptions of the factors and variables that influence police decision making are significantly more accurate.12 We also have learned a great deal more about misconduct by patrol officers. It now is clear that in encounters where it is possible for police patrol officers to choose among a variety of dispositional alternatives, extra-legal criteria (such as demeanor and race) may dominate police exercise of discretion.13 Furthermore, we can see that corruption of patrol officers is present in nearly all police departments that have been studied, although the extent, of corruption is dependent upon the particular department one examines.14 Other studies suggest that unnecessary force by patrol officers is far from uncommon.15

## Purpose of the Book

The fundamental purpose of this book is to provide a sociological perspective on police behavior. It focuses on the results of recent research in the areas of police patrol behavior and misconduct organized from a sociological perspective. The book is intended to be used in several ways. First, the length of the book and its limited focus are intended to encourage adoption in a variety of sociology courses as a supplemental text. Courses such as criminology, social deviance, social problems, the sociology of law, and juvenile delinquency 12 See, for example, Carl Werthman and Irving Piliavin, “Gang Members and the Police,” in David J. Bordua (ed.), The Police: Six Sociological Essays. N ew York: John W iley and Sons, Inc., 1967, pp. 56-98. 13 For a review of this literature see Richard J. Lundman, “The Police Function and the Problem of External Control,” in Emilio C. Viano and Jeffrey H. Reiman (eds.). The Police in Society. Lexington, Mass.: D. C. Heath and Company, 1975, pp. 161-166. 14 See, for example, Julian Roebuck and Thomas Barker, “A Typology of Police Corruption,” Social Problems 21 (April 1974):423-438. 15 Albert J. Reiss, Jr., “Police Brutality—Answers to Key Questions,” Trans-Action (July-August, 1968): 12.

## X / PREFACE

routinely include sections on the police. Instructors teaching these courses should find the book useful. The book also is directed at disciplines related to sociology. Specifically, it is intended for use in Criminal Justice Programs where there is a clear need for comprehensive treatments of police patrol behavior. While this book is not intended to provide its readers with practical guidance, it makes a useful contribution to criminal justice students by broadening their understanding of the law enforcement and criminal justice system. It does so by providing analyses of the societal and organizational forces that influence police patrol behavior, a perspective that should enhance appreciation of the complexities of contemporary policing. Another aspect of the book that lends itself to criminal justice sources is its focus on uniformed, motorized police work. Granting that some criminal justice majors may aspire to supervisory positions, it must still be recognized that future administrators in police departments are generally required to spend a fixed amount of time with the patrol division. This book anticipates the nature of that experience. Finally, this book is intended for use in academically oriented courses devoted solely to the police. Although these courses are not currently numerous, the continued scholarly and societal interest in the police should result in an increase. To assist students in this type of course, additional readings are listed in each chapter.
    Because of these different adoption possibilities, the articles
were selected to maximize student interest and readability. While most articles appeared in social science journals, I avoided those with social science jargon and those that contained intricate or unfamiliar statistical analyses. Additionally, the essays that introduce the two sections of the book as well as the chapter introductions were written with the student reader in mind.
    It also is necessary to acknowledge several debts. I worked
with John Clark and Richard Sykes for four years during the

He “introduced” sociology to Jean Planty Lundman and me over a decade ago as he has to hundreds of students at Beloit, both before and since. He is a gifted scholar and teacher and we owe him a great deal. This is simply one way of expressing our gratitude.

## POLICE BEHAVIOR

## Overview

For many years police patrol officers did their work in relative obscurity. Although a minority of citizens had crime-related contacts with the police and more had contacts in the context of traffic law violations, most citizens knew and cared little about conduct and misconduct by patrol officers. Social scientists also knew and cared little about these matters.1 Beginning with the civil rights movement, however, news reports of one type of police misconduct—unnecessary police violence—thrust police officers into the public spotlight. Public concern and attention increased as police officers appeared on college campuses in connection with the antiwar movement. In these and other situations many people, including governmental officials, did not like what they saw. One consequence was a significant increase in funding for police-related research. Starting in about 1965,12 social scientists expanded their research about police behavior and many, through their studies, became familiar with the nature of conduct and misconduct by police patrol officers. This research has continued and much of it has occasioned revision of previous ideas about the origin and nature of these actions. In the area of police conduct, for instance, it was formerly assumed that police patrol officers were relatively autonomous, responding to their own personal needs or departmental guidelines.3 Based on recent research, however, it now appears nec1. For an exception see W illiam A. W estley, “Violence and the Police,” American Journal of Sociology 59 (1953):3^-42.
2. David Bayley advances this argument in “The Police and Political
Change in Comparative Perspective,” Law and Society Review 6 (1971): 19-112.
3. See W ayne LaFave, Arrest: The Decision to Take a Suspect into Custody. Boston: Little, Brown, 1965.

## 4 / OVERVIEW

essary to suggest that police patrol behavior also reflects the actions and preferences of citizens.4 Similarly, it previously was argued that misconduct by patrol officers was the result of the presence of a few prejudiced or undisciplined officers.5 Recent research, though, suggests that it is necessary to devote some explanatory attention to external opportunities for misconduct as well as to police responses to those opportunities. It also has become evident that it is necessary to explore and to take into account characteristics of police organizations in attempting to develop fuller explanations of police misconduct.6 These changes in ideas about the origins and nature of conduct and misconduct by police patrol officers are the central concerns of this book. In the sections and chapters that follow, each of these types of police behavior is explored from a sociological perspective. We will begin by providing definitions for the key terms used throughout the book.
    Definition of Key Terms
Police patrol behavior refers to the problems, encounters, and decisions routinely characteristic of the patrol experience in large urban areas. During the course of a working shift, police patrol officers confront a wide variety of citizen-initiated problems. These problems involve reactive policing and may include citizen requests for police assistance with barking dogs, automobile accidents, groups of troublesome juveniles, husband-wife disputes, noisy parties, and drunken citizens. A minority of the problems police patrol officers deal with are proactive or police initiated. A car stopped for a traffic law violation illustrates a proactive problem.
4. See Donald J. Black, “Production of Crime Rates,” American Sociological Review 35 (1970): 73 3-747.
5. See August Vollmer, The Police and Modern Society. Berkeley, Calif.:
University of California Press, 1936, pp. 3-4.
6. See Julian Roebuck and Thomas Barker, “A T ypology of Police Corruption,” Social Problems 21 (1974) :423-437.

## OVERVIEW / 5

Many of these problems produce an encounter, or period of face-to-face and generally polite (but constrained) interaction between patrol officers and citizens. In the case of a barking dog, for instance, police patrol officers may talk with a complainant and then with the owner of the barking dog. Of the problems that result in an encounter many require a decision intended to resolve the problem and terminate the encounter. Police patrol officers may warn the owners of barking dogs, tell husbands involved in disputes with their wives to leave, disperse troublesome juveniles, or arrest drunken citizens. Misconduct by police patrol officers includes abuse of discretion, corruption, and use of unnecessary force. Discretion exists when patrol officers are in a position to decide to terminate an encounter in more than one way. In the typical traffic law violation encounter, for example, a police patrol officer is in a position to terminate the encounter by issuing a traffic citation or by simply warning the offending motorist. Abuse of discretion exists when police patrol officers base their decisions on factors, such as race, that are contrary to the democratic norms surrounding contemporary policing, or on factors, such as the behavior or demeanor of the citizen, that are not part of the criminal law. Corruption exists when police patrol officers accept money, goods, or services for actions they are sworn to do anyway, or when police patrol officers accept money, goods, or services for ignoring or tolerating actions they are sworn to invoke legal processes against.7 Corruption may range from acceptance of free or discount meals in exchange for proactive policing to acceptance of money for ignoring gambling operations or the sale of narcotics. Unnecessary force occurs when police patrol officers utilize physical force in encounters v/here alternatives to physical force are available. Unnecessary police force may range from assaults on citizens who offer no physical resistance or threat to a patrol officer, as in assault of a passive handcuffed citizen,
7. This definition follows from one advanced in Lawrence R. Sherman
(ed.), Police Corruption: A Sociological Perspective. Garden City, N.Y.: Anchor Books, 1974, p. 6.

## 6 / OVERVIEW

to the use of a handgun against a citizen who is not presenting a clear and immediate danger to the officer or another citizen. Now that key terms have been defined, we will look at the perspective sociologists use to view events. This will provide a framework to view police behavior.
    A Sociological Perspective
As is true of all scientific disciplines, sociology is a lively arena of dispute, debate, and controversy. This intellectual ferment suggests a discipline that is conscious of the complexities of social phenomena and unwilling to give way to dogmatic interpretation. It also testifies to the existence of different theoretical perspectives and research strategies. Some sociological theorists, for example, believe that primary emphasis should be given to the structure of society and the ways in which structural elements such as social class influence human behavior. In explaining the criminal deviance police patrol officers are called upon to deal with, structuralist Robert K. Merton argues that persons located in the less advantaged segments of our society experience a “definite pressure . . . to engage in nonconforming rather than conforming conduct.”8 Other sociologists assert that it is more logical to view society as a set of collectivities involved in inevitable, but essentially beneficial, conflict. For these sociologists competition for scarce resources such as power, privilege, and wealth constitutes the fabric of society. They also assert that the consequences of this competition are positive in the sense of binding together individuals and groups. Conflict theorist Lewis Coser, for instance, argues that no group or society can be devoid of conflict since both “require disharmony as well as harmony, dissociation as well as association; and conflicts within them are by no means altogether disruptive forces.”9 From this
8. Robert K. Merton, Social Theory and Social Structure. N ew York:
Macmillan Publishing Company, 1968, p. 186.
9. Lewis Coser, The Functions of Social Conflict. N ew York: The Free
Press, 1956, p. 31.

## OVERVIEW / 7

perspective criminal deviance is one result of conflict in contemporary society. Still other sociologists ignore the structure of society and its intergroup conflicts in favor of the study of face-to-face interaction. Edwin H. Sutherland believed that structural- and conflict-oriented explanations of crime are inadequate. Sutherland suggested that we instead examine patterns of association in small groups since “the principal part of the learning of criminal behavior occurs within intimate personal groups.”101 In the area of theory, then, there exists dispute and disagreement over which theoretical tradition or paradigm best explains human action generally and criminal deviance specifically. For the most part there are three theoretical perspectives: the structural functional, conflict, and interactionist. Clearly, the apparent absence of agreement confounds the task of developing a sociological perspective on police patrol behavior. However, perspective is not the only issue under debate within the discipline of sociology. Research strategies also are marked by controversy. Some sociologists assert that information provided by interview and questionnaire is adequate.11 Other sociologists suggest that sound theory can be built only by personal observation of social conduct.12 Still other sociologists contend that laboratory and field experiments are necessary ingredients of any overall research strategy.13 Therefore, in a very real sense, there also is no single research strategy acceptable to all sociologists. Despite the multiplicity of perspectives and methods within sociology, there are areas of overlap and agreement. Common

10. Edwin H. Sutherland and Donald R. Cressey, Criminology, 9th edition, Philadelphia: J. B. Lippincott Company, 1974, p. 75.
11. See, for example, Mark Benney and Everett C. Hughes, “Of Sociology and the Interview,” American Journal of Sociology 62 (1956):
137-142.
12. See, for example, Raymond Gold, “Roles in Sociological Field Observations,” Social Forces 36 (1958):217-223.
13. See, for example, Darrell J. Steffensmeier and Robert M. Terry (eds.),
Examining Deviance Experimentally. Sherman Oaks, Calif.: Alfred Publishing Co., Inc., 1975.

## 8 / OVERVIEW

to nearly all contemporary sociological theories of human action is a focus on extra-individualistic, or social, forces and events. The sociologist who adopts a structural orientation seeks to explain social behavior in terms of the interrelationships of social institutions. Conflict-oriented sociologists emphasize social competition among groups. Even sociologists who study face-to-face interaction devote primary explanatory attention to such extra-individualistic factors as social rules, norms, and expectations. Although none of these sociologists ignores the individual, all seek explanations of human action in terms of factors other than the unique characteristics of particular persons. In the area of research strategies there also exists a sociological common ground. Minimally the sociological interviewer, observer, and experimenter agree that the research goal should be an accumulation of data (and knowledge) as free of bias and distortion as possible. In addition, most researchers use techniques whose theory and implementation are generally known. Consequently, other sociologists who wish to use or critique them have public access. Common to nearly all contemporary research methods, then, is a concern with the development of reliable and valid knowledge about particular phenomena. These common theoretical and methodological concerns can be termed a “sociological imagination,” a perspective that can be applied in the study of a variety of phenomena—including police behavior.14 Sociologist C. W right Mills described the benefits of developing and applying a sociological imagination:
    T h e sociological im agination is the m ost fru itfu l form o f . . .
    self-consciousness. B y its use . . . [p eop le] . . . w h ose m entalities have sw ep t o n ly a series o f lim ited orbits o ften com e to feel
    as if suddenly aw akened in a house w h ich th ey had on ly supposed them selves to be fam iliar. C orrectly or in correctly, they
    o ften com e to feel that th ey can n o w p rovide them selves w ith
    adequate sum m ations, cohesive assessments, com prehensive orien14. C. W right Mills, The Sociological Imagination. N ew York: Grove
Press, Inc., 1959.

## OVERVIEW / 9

tations. Older decisions that once appeared sound now seem to them products of a mind unaccountably dense. Their capacity for astonishment is made lively again. T h ey acquire a new way of thinking, they experience a transvaluation of values: in a word, by their reflection and by their sensibility, they realize the cultural meaning of the social sciences.15
Sensitized by Mills’ promise of the benefits to be derived from application of a sociological imagination, the basic purpose of this book is to develop a sociological perspective on police patrol behavior.

## Police Behavior: A Sociological Perspective

Assuming Mills is correct, what can we expect to learn from applying a sociological perspective to police patrol behavior? Minimally we should expect to come away from our efforts with a revised and expanded understanding of police patrol behavior. This section will enable us to anticipate or preview the nature of that revision and expansion. In studying police behavior, often an initial tendency is to concentrate primarily on the individual patrol officer. This tendency develops since the patrol officer’s uniform, weapons, and powers of arrest yield an apearance of relative independence and autonomy. Arthur Niederhoffer, a former New York City police officer turned professor, observes:
The policeman is a “Rorschach” in uniform as he patrols his beat. H is occupational accounterments—shield, nightstick, gun, and summons book—clothe him in a mantle of symbolism that stimulates fantasy and projection. Children identify with him in the perennial game of “cops and robbers.” Teenagers in autos stiffen with compulsive rage or anxiety at the sight of the patrol car.16

15. Mills, The Sociological Imagination, pp. 7-8.
16. Arthur Niederhoffer, Behind the Shield. Garden City, N.Y.: Anchor
Books, 1969, p. 1.

## 10 / OVERVIEW

Our newly acquired sociological imagination encourages us to look beyond this appearance of autonomy and the individualistic emphasis that it produces. It encourages discovery of new ideas about the social forces and events that shape police behavior and misconduct. It also permits revision and expansion of existing explanations of these actions. The benefits are several. First, when we look beyond the apparent autonomy of the patrol officer we discover that police behavior also is determined by citizens. W e learn that most police-citizen encounters are initiated by citizens who telephone the police dispatch room. We also learn that a meaningful portion of police behavior occurs in response to the actions of the citizens present during an encounter. Furthermore, we discover that certain police decisions reflect the actions and preferences of citizens. One basic theme of this book is that police patrol behavior is determined by citizens as voell as by patrol officers. Second, by applying a sociological perspective we discover that misconduct by police patrol officers is a complex phenomenon involving the interaction or convergence of at least two elements: illegitimate opportunities and informal departmental operating rules that encourage or tolerate officers who take advantage of these opportunities. More specifically, we learn that community and departmental forces combine to account for organized misconduct by police patrol officers. This contrasts markedly with earlier explanations, which emphasized individual character traits as explanatory factors.17 A second basic theme of the book is that misconduct by patrol officers is a function of community and departmental characteristics.
     Plan of the Book
The plan of the book follows from these two basic themes. The first section of the book details the ways in which police
17. August Vollmer was a leading advocate of this approach. For a discussion o f this see Gene F. Carte and Elaine H . Carte, Police Reform in
the United States: The Era of August Vollmer, 1905-1932. Berkeley, Calif.: University of California Press, 1975).

## OVERVIEW / 11

and citizens together shape police patrol behavior. In the three chapters in this section specific attention is given to the influence citizens have on the problems police patrol officers handle, the encounter, and the decision a police officer makes in an effort to resolve or terminate the encounter. The second section of the book examines the ways in which community and departmental characteristics relate to the emergence of misconduct by police patrol officers. In the three chapters contained in this section specific attention is given to abuse of discretion, corruption, and use of unnecessary force. Each section and chapter of the book begins with an introduction. The section introductions provide a basic sociological perspective on the issues to be addressed. The chapter introductions preview the readings that follow.

## Police
## Conduct

The basic theme of this first section is that citizens and patrol officials together shape police patrol behavior. This theme will be advanced in the context of examinations of the kinds of problems police patrol officers seek to resolve, the interaction or discussion that occurs during police-citizen encounters, and the dispositional decisions reached by patrol officers.

## Problems

Although it might appear that police patrol officers initiate the majority of encounters with citizens, the truth of the matter is that most patrol work is reactive rather than proactive. Police patrol officers are legally constrained from proactive observation of private places such as homes or apartments unless they have reason to believe that a felony has been or is about to be committed. They are consensually constrained from routine proactive observation of semi-public places such as bars or restaurants by the uneasiness their presence causes proprietors and customers.1As a consequence, proactive police patrol work is restricted to public places such as sidewalks and streets, and it is only infrequently productive of serious criminality. The Federal Bureau of Investigation, for instance, reports that a burglary occurs once every 10 seconds and a robbery once every 78 seconds.2 Yet it has been estimated that a police patrol
1. For a discussion of this see W illiam J. Chambliss and John T . Liell,
“The Legal Process and the Community Setting/’ Crime and Delinquency 12 (1966):310-317.
2. Federal Bureau of Investigation, Uniform Crime Report , 1917. W ashington, D.C.: U.S. Government Printing Office, 1978, p. 68.

## 14 / POLICE CONDUCT

officer will witness, on the average, a burglary in progress once in every three months of patrol and a robbery in progress once every fourteen years.3 Contemporary police organizations are, therefore, highly dependent upon citizen reports of criminal incidents and other situations requiring police presence. Although the figures vary slightly depending upon the particular study one consults, it appears that approximately three-quarters of all police-citizen encounters are initiated by citizens who use the telephone to call the police. If police-initiated traffic stop encounters are excluded from the total of police-citizen contacts, the proportion of citizen-initiated encounters approaches 90 percent.4 This dependence upon citizen input has a profound influence on police organizations and police behavior. First, police reliance upon citizens’ reports of criminal incidents has given citizens partial control over official crime and arrest statistics. Recent research results indicate that only half of the criminal incidents detected by citizens are ever reported to the police.5 In addition, by their willingness or unwillingness to remain at the scene of an incident they have reported to police, complainants determine whether or not a report will be written and therefore whether an arrest can ever be made.6 Second, police dependence upon citizen reports of incidents requiring police presence has made the telephone and dispatch room the center of contemporary police organizations. In this room telephone consoles are constantly attended to and dispatchers are available to direct radio-equipped patrol cars to the scenes of incidents reported by citizens. Jonathan Rubin3. The President’s Commission on Law Enforcement and Administration of Justice, The Police. Washington, D.C.: U.S. Government Printing Office, 1967, p. 14.

## POLICE CONDUCT / 15

stein describes the operation of the telephone and dispatch center of the Philadelphia police department:
The dispatcher’s major responsibilities are to interpret incoming calls and to dispatch assignments to the cars on his network. W hen one of the phones at the console rings, the “phone man” makes out a card containing the address of the caller and the nature of the complaint. T he card is handed to the dispatcher, who sits before a lighted panel marked with the numbers of all the cars and wagons assigned to his division and a detailed map with district and sector boundaries clearly marked. H e punches the card on a time clock and assigns the “job” to a car. H e then files the card in a slot above the lighted panel reserved for that particular car and flips a switch turning off the light under the car’s number on the panel. W hen the officer reports back to the dispatcher that he has completed the assignment, the card is again time-punched and the light turned on.7
Police dependence upon citizen use of the telephone also has had a profound effect on the nature of police patrol work. In one sense the radio for the patrol officer is the equivalent of the factory worker’s assembly line. In high-crime areas especially the radio is a constant source of work for the patrol officer. And, the patrol officer’s superiors and peers require that radioed work assignments be accepted.8 There are several differences, however, between the work experiences of the police patrol officer and those of the factory worker. One is the diversity characteristic of contemporary police patrol work.9 Unlike the factory worker who endlessly repeats the same task, the police patrol officer experiences

instance, a radio call can be ignored and then later be explained by a report of a traffic stop or residence check. However, patrol officers cannot do this regularly since administrators become irritated and colleagues become angry at the extra burden they must carry.

## 16 / POLICE CONDUCT

change in the nature of the tasks performed. Patrol officers may be called upon to write a report of a criminal incident, check someone’s well-being, investigate a suspicious person, and quiet a barking dog. Then, without warning, the emergency tone on the squad’s radio is heard and calls such as the following are put out: “man with a gun,” “shots fired,” “officer needs help,” or “silent alarm.” These “heavy” calls lend excitement and danger to police patrol work. They also provide the ultimate rationale for the existence of the patrol officer:
     T h e heavy ones are ultimately our only rationale for being. You
     could give most of what w e do around here to any idiot who
     could put up w ith the insanity that passes for civilized conduct.
     But you know that old John Q. Citizen isn’t going to put his ass
     on the line [for a heavy call]. . . .10
Police patrol work, then, is characterized by diversity and, occasionally, excitement and danger. Another difference between police work and factory work is the considerable autonomy of the patrol officer. To a great extent the work of the patrol officer is unsupervised and to a lesser extent it is unsupervisable. It is this autonomy that returns some degree of control to patrol officers and allows them to shape, with citizens, the problems or agenda of patrol work. Patrol officers can partially escape citizen control by becoming more proactive.11 Patrol officers have ultimate control over the ratio of reactive to proactive problems since they can choose to initiate a greater proportion of problems. Car and pedestrian checks represent an almost endless source of proactive work for patrol officers. Furthermore, patrol officers can and do “prioritize”12 the
10. From John Van Maanen, “Rookie Cops and Rookie Managers,” The
Wharton Magazine 1:1 (Fall 1976):52.
11. For a discussion and illustration see Richard J. Lundman, Richard E.
Sykes, and John P. Clark, “Police Control of Juveniles: A Replication,” Journal of Research in Crime and Delinquency 15 (January 1978) :78.
12. The author is grateful to Peter K. Manning for calling this to my
attention.

## POLICE CONDUCT / 17

calls they receive. Almost without exception, “heavy” calls receive first priority. For instance, two officers were helping a parent search for a lost child when their portable police radio informed them of an automobile chase. They immediately broke off the search, ran to their squad, and joined in the chase, leaving a bewildered parent standing alone. They returned shortly thereafter but the parent had already found the child.13 In another illustrative situation two officers found a young adult drinking beer in his car. He was quite drunk and the officers decided to arrest him. They handcuffed him and placed him in the back seat of the squad car. As the officers started the drive downtown, a “robbery in progress” call was put out. The officer who was driving immediately pulled to the curb while the officer in the back seat with the “prisoner,” took off the handcuffs, pushed the young man out the door, and “unarrested” him saying, “walk home, don’t drive.” They then responded with emergency speed and lights, but no siren,14 to the robbery call. Lastly, even though they are called to the scene of an incident by a citizen, police patrol officers determine the way in which they respond. For heavy calls emergency speed and lights are utilized and, if the officers are a considerable distance away or if getting there rather than apprehension is of prime importance (e.g., “officer needs help”), the siren also is used. For many other calls, however, police patrol officers respond in a deliberate manner with the hope that the problem and the citizen will be gone by the time they arrive. In the context of domestic or husband-wife disputes, for instance, it was my experience that “police responded without emergency speed, signal lights, and siren, often proceeding . . . in a deliberate manner with the hope that the domestic . . . would be settled

13. This example and the next are from “Midwest City.” See Lundman,
et al., “Police Control,” for a description of the entire project.
14. A siren would have alerted the alleged violator that police were on
their way. For another description of this practice see George Kirkham, Signal "Zero. N ew York: Ballantine Books, 1977, p. 51.

## 18 / POLICE CONDUCT

prior to their arrival.15 Other researchers have advanced essentially similar observations.16 What, then, can be said about the ways in which citizens and police patrol officers together shape the problems characteristic of patrol work? At least two summary points are in order. First, citizens largely, but not solely, determine the types of problems police patrol officers work with. The nature of police patrol work rests largely in the hands of citizens. Second, within the constraints imposed on their work by citizens, police patrol officers determine the priority of the problems they deal with and the ways in which they respond. The perceived importance and pace of patrol work rests largely in the hands of patrol officers.
     Encounters
As compared to the direct control citizens exert in determining the problems patrol officers deal with, citizen control over the actual flow, or trajectory, of interaction during an encounter is significantly less direct. In one sense police patrol officers rather than citizens control the encounter. Self-protection is the first priority of the patrol officer arriving at the scene of an encounter.17 The officer initially acts to ensure that none.of the citizens present have weapons on their persons or within their reach. Generally this is accomplished visually and by controlling the physical locations of the citizens present. If people or the premises are at all suspicious, however, an officer rarely hesitates to undertake a search of either, or both. Establishing respect for police authority is the second priority of the patrol officer involved in an encounter with citi15. Richard J. Lundman, “Domestic Police-Citizen Encounters,” Journal of Police Science and Administration 2 (March 1974): 25.
16. Rubinstein, City Police, p. 97.
17. Rubinstein, City Police, pp. 227-266.

## POLICE CONDUCT / 19

zens.18 Police patrol officers enter encounters with the expectation that citizens will be polite and, to some extent, respectful in their interaction with them. This often takes the form of an “attitudes test” which, on the basis of the available evidence, appears to be a commonly encountered characteristic of large urban police organizations.19 Sociologist Rodney Stark describes the use of the “attitudes test” by Los Angeles patrol officers:
Virtually all studies of the Los Angeles Department report that the attitudes test is a common part of L.A. police argot. . . . The attitudes test . . . indicates that in police-citizen interactions the police impose certain standards o f deference and respect and failure to meet this test merits reprisals . . . use of violence, harrassment, or bringing of chicken shit citations and charges.20
Use of the attitudes test by patrol officers is thus another way in which they, rather than citizens, control police-citizen interactions. “Normalization”21 is the third priority of patrol officers. Normalization is the process whereby an officer establishes that a particular encounter is representative of the hundreds or thousands of similar encounters experienced in the past. Normalization is important to an officer for at least two reasons. First, it allows an officer to reliably predict citizen behavior during the course of an encounter. If an officer can establish that a particular husband-wife dispute is similar to other husband-wife disputes, the officer can expect both parties to be
18. For a discussion see Richard E. Sykes and John P. Clark, “Deference
Exchange in Police-Civilian Encounters,” American Journal of Sociology 81 (November 1975): 584-600.
19. For example, James Q. W ilson, Varieties of Police Behavior. Cambridge, Mass.: Harvard University Press, 1968, pp. 36-37.
20. Rodney Stark, Police Riots. Belmont, Calif.: Wadsworth Publishing
Company, 1972, p. 61. The order of the wording of the original statement has been altered.
21. The discussion of normalization expands upon one presented by
David Sudnow, “Normal Crimes: Sociological Features of the Penal Code in a Public Defender Office,” Social Problems 12 (W inter 1965): 255-276.

## 20 / POLICE CONDUCT

agitated and generally equally responsible for the particular incident. The officer also can predict that the wife will claim to be the victim and ask or demand that the husband be seen as the violator.22 Additionally, normalization permits utilization of the decision-making guidelines or “recipes for action”23 used in normal encounters. If an encounter can be normalized, the officer can be relatively confident of the general nature of the interaction and ultimate resolution of the problem. For instance, if a husband-wife dispute can be normalized, the experienced patrol officer would try to separate the combatants, listen more patiently to the complaints of the wife, and suggest that they both forget the problem for now. If that suggestion is unworkable or unacceptable, the husband is then told to leave. Rarely is formal action such as arrest undertaken, except as a last resort, since the officer knows that in the typical husbandwife dispute both parties will object.24 Normalization, in short, makes routine police work possible. However, normalization can occur only if an officer “knows” a citizen. But in most encounters the particular citizens present are not personally known to the officer. As a consequence, citizens must provide patrol officers with the information that permits normalization. It is in this way that citizens residually shape the trajectory of police-citizen encounters. Police patrol officers generally arrive at the scene of an encounter with only a fragmentary description of the problem and an educated guess as to the types of persons who might be present. Indeed, all the responding patrol officer typically pos22. See Lundman, “Domestic Police-Citizen Encounters.”
23. Thomas Scheff discusses “recipes for action” in his description of the
socialization experiences of new organizational members. By this phrase he means what is typically or generally done to deviants by organization members. See Thomas J. Scheff, “Typifications in the Diagnostic Practices of Rehabilitation Agencies,” in Marvin B. Sussman (ed.), Sociology and Rehabilitation. Cleveland, Ohio: American Sociological Association, 1966, pp. 139-144.
24. See Kirkham, Sig?ial Zero , pp. 66-67.

## POLICE CONDUCT / 21

sesses is the address of a vaguely described problem. Fortunately, some citizens present at the scene of an encounter feel compelled to talk, if for no other reason than to explain their presence. If they are reluctant or situationally tongue tied, though, patrol officers prevail upon them to amplify terse radio descriptions and to account for their possible relation to the incident. Rarely is extensive information collected, but the officer continues until able to place the problem by reference to calls typically received in that area. Citizens, therefore, provide patrol officers with the information fundamental to normalization of the encounter. Once identification of the problem has occurred, further normalization is dependent upon the actions and, especially, the attitudes of the citizens present. Given a typical problem, it is generally possible for an experienced officer to predict how the citizens present will look and act. Citizens who are publicly drunk, for example, are expected to be poorly dressed and somewhat belligerent. Traffic law violators are expected to be surprised and somewhat defensive, while felony victims are expected to be frightened. All citizens are expected to be reasonably polite and somewhat respectful of police authority. Citizens who meet these expectations permit complete normalization of an encounter, since confirmation of expectations makes routine policing possible. The only task that remains is informing naive or slow citizens of the inevitable. Once normalization has taken place, the vast majority of police interaction is polite. Moreover, only rarely do encounters involve displays of violence by patrol officers or unintentional losses of verbal control.25 When these relatively infrequent actions occur they generally are triggered by citizens who blocked normalization by flunking the “attitudes test.”26 We shall return to this issue in the second half of the book. What we find, in sum, is that police patrol officers enter encounters with these priorities: self-protection, establishing re25. See Lundman, “Domestic Police-Citizen Encounters.”
26. See Stark, Police Riots, p. 61.

## 22 / POLICE CONDUCT

spect for police authority, and normalization. Patrol officers, therefore, have ultimate control over their encounters with citizens. However, much of what actually occurs during an encounter is determined by citizens. Patrol officers are dependent upon citizens for information and they are keenly sensitive to their attitudes. Citizen control over encounters is thus present, but it is indirect and occurs within constraints imposed by police.
     Decisions
Until recently it has been commonplace to observe that police patrol work is largely discretionary.27 By this it was meant that police officers possess extraordinary freedom in deciding how to dispose of or deal with a problem. Frequently associated with this observation were accusations that police officers abuse this discretion by treating black citizens differently than white citizens and lower-class citizens differently than middleclass citizens.28 Although these accusations are sometimes true (see Chapter 4), it is necessary to recognize that citizens possess and exercise discretion too. It also is necessary to recognize that police exercise of discretion is limited by a variety of factors other than citizen exercise of discretion. We begin by reviewing the steps leading up to police contact with a citizen. It will be recalled that police patrol work is largely reactive. Citizens determine whether or not a police officer will be in a position to exercise discretion by their willingness to call the police. As stated earlier, evidence indicates that citizens elect not to call the police half the time. Citizens further determine or constrain police exercise of discretion by their willingness to remain at the scene of an encounter until the police arrive. If a citizen calls the police but then leaves, fails to appear or
27. See, for example, W ilson, Varieties of Police Behavior, pp. 16-27.
28. See, for example, Irving Piliavin and Scott Briar, “Police Encounters
with Juveniles,” American Journal of Sociology 70 (September 1964): 206-214.

## POLICE CONDUCT / 23

reappear once the police arrive, or otherwise deliberately avoids contact with the police, there is little an officer can do. These “GOA’s” (gone on arrivals) represent further exercise of discretion by citizens. All we need to note, therefore, is that for every encounter in which it appears that it is possible for a police officer to exercise discretion, there is at least one other that was eliminated by citizen exercise of discretion. When citizens are present, police exercise of discretion is limited by the nature of the problem, police bureaucracy, and citizen preferences. In fact, if we take discretion to refer to an absolute choice between or among two or more mutually exclusive dispositional alternatives, then police patrol officers deal with more problems where only one outcome is possible than problems where several are possible. Service requests involving sick calls, lock-outs, and nonvehicular accidents represent situations where the nature of the problem limits police exercise of discretion. In these situations police patrol officers may alter the quality of the services rendered, but save for the most obnoxious of citizens, they have little choice but to render some degree of service. Alleged felony encounters with a suspect and credible witness or a suspect and sound evidence represent situations where police bureaucracy limits exercise of discretion. Most police organizations and officers are evaluated by reference to felony arrests. Consequently, only naive or very fortunate officers are in a position to routinely release felony suspects. It is probably more accurate to note that meaningful choices simply are not present in this type of encounter.29 In encounters revolving around alleged misdemeanors a legal arrest is possible only if the officer witnesses the incident, or if a citizen witnessing the offense calls the police and is willing to make a citizen’s arrest. Since citizens witness the majority of misdemeanors and because many are not willing to

29. The single exception that comes to m y mind is the officer who stops
a juvenile in a stolen car. Although I did not witness an outright release in this circumstance, officers informed me that this is done on occasion.

## 24 / POLICE CONDUCT

inconvenience themselves by reporting a misdemeanor or by making a citizen’s arrest, it should be clear that citizens possess the balance of discretion in this circumstance. Consequently, it is important to recognize that it is only in a minority of encounters that patrol officers are actually free to choose between or among two or more mutually exclusive alternatives. Specifically, absolute discretion exists primarily in encounters involving technical violations of the law wherein suspects are present and other citizens generally are absent. These types of encounters include contacts with juveniles, public drunkenness offenders, and traffic law violators. The basic summary point is that patrol officers are generally constrained in their selection of dispositional alternatives. The factors that limit police discretion include citizen actions occurring in advance of and during an encounter, as well as the nature of the problem, police bureaucracy, and the law. Absolute discretion is present in only a minority of encounters.
     Summary and Conclusions
The essential argument advanced here and in the three chapters that follow is that police patrol behavior is shaped by citizens as well as patrol officers. The kinds of problems police officers are called upon to deal with, the nature of police behavior during encounters, and the dispositional decisions advanced by patrol officers are reflective of the actions and preferences of citizens as well as patrol officers. As is true of all generalizations, however, important exceptions exist. Police officers and their administrative superiors can increase the proactive share of patrol work by increasing traffic or pedestrian stops, working prostitution more aggressively, or more actively patrolling areas known to be frequented by chronic public drunkenness offenders. Similarly, patrol officers can elect to take complete control of their contacts with citizens, thereby placing citizens in even more of a secondary position. Finally, patrol officers can occasionally

## POLICE CONDUCT / 25

choose to ignore bureaucratic pressures or citizen preferences. In these and other ways, police patrol behavior escapes citizen control. The final point, then, is that police patrol work is best understood as a joint product of the actions of patrol officers and citizens. Exceptions exist but, in general, mutual rather than independent action characterizes police patrol behavior. It is this understanding that receives detailed attention in the chapters that follow.
I / T h e Problems
## / PREVIEW OF T H E SELECTIONS

In analyzing the kinds of problems police patrol officers are routinely called upon to deal with three points are most important. First, citizens perceive that many situations require that authority or force of some type be utilized. That is why citizens call the police. Second, police officers are called upon to deal with a wide variety of problems. Third, most of these problems are identified by citizens who use the telephone to call the police. These three points occupy our attention in this first chapter. In the first selection, “The Police Charge,” Egon Bittner suggests that the police privilege to exercise non-negotiable coercive force is what prompts citizens to call the police about a variety of problems. During the course of a working shift police patrol officers may be called upon to deal with barking dogs; automobile accidents; groups of juveniles; husband-wife disputes; noisy parties, radios, and televisions; drunken citizens; and occasionally, criminal incidents. Bittner observes that what lends unity to each of these situations is that forceful actions are required of and undertaken by patrol officers: owners are warned; traffic re-routed; juveniles dispersed; husbands sent out of the house; parties, radios, and televisions quieted; and, infrequently, citizens arrested. Citizens call the police because of their perception that authority or force of some type is required. Selections 2 and 3, “Policing” by Joseph Livermore and “Police Patrol Work: A Comparative Prespective” by Richard J. Lundman, suggest the wide variety of problems patrol officers are called upon to deal with. These selections also estab-

## THE PROBLEMS / 27

lish the extent to which police are dependent upon citizen reports of incidents requiring police presence. Livermore, a law professor, spent three weeks riding in police patrol cars in Minneapolis. “Policing” describes the encounters he observed on two of those days. Notice should be taken of the diversity of the problems handled and the fact that nearly all patrol activities were initiated by citizens. Lundman’s “Police Patrol Work: A Comparative Perspective” describes police work in five different police departments. Although there was some variation dependent upon type of department, slightly over two-thirds of all the encounters were citizen initiated. If police-initiated traffic stops are excluded, nearly 90 percent of all encounters were citizen initiated. This establishes that most police-citizen encounters are initiated by citizens. Missing from these selections, however, is evidence that police patrol officers determine the priority of the problems they deal with and the ways in which they respond. It is important to remember, therefore, that while citizens largely determine the nature of patrol work, the perceived importance and pace of patrol work rests largely in the hands of patrol officers.
