W hy is this so? Let us look at the dimensions of our system of justice as it applies to police use of force. The reader should be aware that it is the system, rather than the police, that is the focus of critical examination here. Many police killings are justifiable and police officers are often in danger, but it is with the unjustifiable killings that the system fails.

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 315

Laws Regarding Police Use of Force What are the legal limitations on police use of force? States vary, but there is a general thrust of law throughout the nation (Chapman, 1967). A police officer is clearly justified in using deadly force when it is necessary to save himself or another person from death or grave bodily harm. This is so in any situation. He is generally not permitted to use deadly force to protect himself or others from assaults which are not likely to have serious results. This basic “defense of life” rule is sometimes misused. In some areas it seems to be standard practice for the police to report that a fleeing unarmed person, later killed, had moved his hand toward his belt or had a shiny object in his hand. Such a report alone is accepted as justification for the use of firearms—even if the person is fleeing.
    Another general rule is that an officer may not use deadly
force to effect the arrest of a person suspected of being a misdemeanant, someone who has committed a minor crime. In the case of an escaping misdemeanant, the value of human life seems to supersede the importance of immediate apprehension. However, it is noteworthy that in some jurisdictions the very act of fleeing is defined as a felony; under such a law an officer is permitted to use all possible force, including firearms, to prevent the escape of a person fleeing after an arrest has been made, no matter what the original offense.
    One example of the use of this guideline occurred in the
case of T.O., who was killed on December 28, 1968, by Patrolman G.K. in Prince Georges County, Maryland. T.O. had driven to the Mount Pleasant police station at about 1:45 a .ai ., parked his car, and told the police he was too drunk to drive and wanted them to drive him home. He refused a police offer to call a cab for him, became boisterous and unruly, and was charged with being drunk and disorderly. As he was being taken to a cell he hit officer G.K. and ran outside to the parking lot. G.K. followed, fired two warning shots and then a third shot which hit T.O. in the neck; he died in a hospital intensive care ward six days later.

## 316 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

G.K. was suspended and the case presented to the county grand jury. The state’s attorney noted that the patrolman had “apparently deviated” from the prosecutor’s guidelines on police use of firearms and that there may have been a “violation of the law.” Maryland law indicated that a policeman should avoid firing at a person fleeing from arrest on a misdemeanor such as a disorderly conduct charge. The grand jury, however, brought no indictments. A police board was convened to evaluate patrolman G.K.’s action and concluded that “O. became a felon when he escaped from K.’s custody, regardless of the charge on which he had been arrested.” The board quoted Article 27, Section 139, of the Annotated Code of Maryland, which holds that: “If any offender legally detained and confined in the penitentiary, jail, . . . or station, escapes he shall be guilty of a felony.” They added that police regulations permit firing at fleeing felons. On February 26, 1969, Patrolman G.K. was reinstated with back pay and vacation time. There are few statutory limits on police in dealing with people who are defined as felons. In most states it is lawful for the police to risk killing when necessary to effect the arrest or to prevent the escape of a person guilty of a felony if no other means of preventing flight is reasonably available. The laws of states vary; some say that the person has to be a known felon, that the felony has to be seen, or the person has to be known to have committed a felony. Others indicate that reasonable suspicion of the carrying out of a felony is adequate for defining the person as a felon and therefore maximum force can be used if other methods for apprehension are not available. The fleeing felon rule is often stretched beyond recognition by the police and other authorities to justify a police homicide. An example was the case of R.M., 19 years old, who was killed by Riverside, California, motorcycle Patrolman J.W. on February 7, 1967. Early in the afternoon, J.W. received a call reporting a speeding and recklessly driven Chevrolet; he went to investigate but could not locate the car. A couple of hours later, after a second call, he spotted the car and pursued it. The car was going fast for that street and J.W. turned on his red light; about a tenth of a mile later the car slowed and pulled

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 317

over. Two young people were in the car. W. asked the driver, R.M., for his driver’s license; he said he didn’t have one. Patrolman W. told both young men to get out of the car and told them they were under arrest. He had them place their hands on the hood of the car and searched them, both young people being cooperative; there were no weapons. W. then began to suspect the car might be stolen and reached through the window for the registration holder. The young men ran. He chased them, yelled for them to stop, said he would shoot, fired a shot into the air, kept running after one young man, and finally killed him. A 15-member jury unanimously decided after 20 minutes’ deliberation that the killing was justifiable under the law. The deputy district attorney who conducted the inquest instructed the jury that “the privilege of justification” extends to a situation in which a law enforcement officer has reasonable cause to believe the suspect had committed a felony, even though the suspect, who was shot while “fleeing justice,” or “resisting arrest,” had not been charged formally with a felony. The district attorney later announced his decision not to bring criminal charges against J.W. Samuel Chapman, who did the major research on police use of firearms for the 1966-67 President’s Commission on Law Enforcement and the Administration of Justice, wrote:
T h e rule that an officer m ay, if necessary, take the life o f a felon in effecting an arrest seem s linked to the ancient law under w h ich all felonies w ere punishable b y death. In recen t years, how ever, various legislative bodies have introduced such n o n -violen t o ffenses as statutory rape, larceny, sod om y, extortion, perjury, fo rgery, adultery, bigam y, incest, pandering, to our crim inal codes and afforded them fe lo n y status. In consequence, the traditional con cep t that an officer m ay use w hatever force is necessary to e ffect the arrest o f a k n ow n felo n has becom e com plicated and open to ridicule. (1967, p. 27)
But the excesses of the fleeing felon rule are not limited to felonies; it is used to justify the killing of a misdemeanant, especially since suspicion alone is an adequate defense. Such use,

## 318 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

particularly in light of the abolition of the death penalty in our nation, makes the killings thoroughly incongruous with our values and legal structure. Moreover, one can question the constitutionality of the usual justifiable homicide statute. An officer can justifiably kill a person if he has probable cause to believe him to be a fleeing felon. Execution, then, is authorized on probable cause alone, and all the constitutionally defined due process safeguards, which at the minimum require proof of guilt before any punishment can be levied, are removed from the affected citizen. It must be emphasized that the suspicion of a felony, even enough suspicion to result in arrest, does not mean that the individual arrested and accused is guilty. In the many situations where police fire at “fleeing felons,” the policemen are taking into their own hands the position of accuser, judge, jury, and executioner. After all, a sizeable number of those arrested in the United States by police officers acting on the reasonable belief that a felony has been committed are released with no charges brought against them.

## Police Administration Rules Regarding Firearms

In addition to the statutory limitations on the use of firearms by police officers, many police departments have regulations of their own. These vary widely. The President’s Commission understated the case in its conclusion: “In most cities police officers receive too little guidance as to when firearms may be drawn and used” (1967, p. 119). Firearm policies in some cities are contained in their entirety by such statements as: “Leave the gun in the holster until you intend to use it,” and, “It is left to the discretion of each individual officer when and how to shoot” (Chapman, 1967). In some cities, guidelines are both clear and humane. A Los Angeles officer cannot use deadly force to protect himself from an attack “not likely to have serious results,” nor can he shoot at a suspected felony offender who refuses the command to halt or at a known felony offender who acts similarly

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 319

“except under compelling circumstances.” A police officer is commanded not to shoot at even the most dangerous juvenile offender unless his own or another person’s life is imperiled. Officers are warned that regardless of the legal justification for firing at a suspect, the policeman’s “basic responsibility is to protect the public” and officers “should be particularly cautious when firing under conditions that would subject bystanders to injury or death” (The Los Angeles Times, 1965). Of course, it is difficult to know how many police officers are limited in their use of guns by such guidelines. What is apparent, however, is that more civilians are killed by police officers in Los Angeles than in any other city (Kobler, 1975), that very many of the killings are in obvious deviance from the police regulations, and finally that few police officers are penalized, at least as far as the public can know.
    Penalties against police officers who have killed citizens
probably occur most often as a consequence of internal police procedures. These are usually, but not always, secret. Some nonsecret discipline has followed public trials of police killers who were acquitted. One was the trial of Officer M.O. in San Francisco, who killed G.B., a 28-year-old black man, in September of 1968. Officer M.O. was off duty, had had a good bit to drink, and after an altercation over a minor incident, killed B., a latecomer to the scene who had attempted to knock the gun out of M.O.’s hands with a stick (a rung from a chair). After a long trial and a hung jury, the police officer was acquitted. Nevertheless, the San Francisco Police Commission held hearings related to charges of M.O.’s unofficerlike conduct. The three-man commission unanimously voted to fire Officer M.O. from the force, finding him guilty of “unofficerlike conduct and breach of the peace,” findings based in part on the policeman’s racial epithets that day.
    Patrolman R.M. killed a 25-year-old black man, L.H., in October of 1965 in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania. L.H. was one of
about a dozen men shooting craps on the street when the police tried to raid the game. R.M. fired a warning shot at the running L.H., something that was against police regulations, and then shot again and killed him. M. claimed that the second

## 320 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

sh o t w a s an a c c id e n t, th a t h e h ad slip p e d o n d iffic u lt and r o u g h g r o u n d . H e w a s c h a r g e d an d a c q u itte d o f m u r d e r and in v o lu n ta r y m a n sla u g h ter. A f t e r th e a cq u itta l, a p o lic e trial b oard r e c o m m e n d e d a su sp e n sio n o f six m o n th s fo r P a tro lm a n R .M . T h e y fo u n d “ th a t h e u se d an d u n n e c e ssa r ily d isc h a r g e d firearm s, th a t h e d iso b e y e d sp e c ific o rd ers o f th e su p e r in te n d e n t o f p o lic e and o th e r su p e r io r o fficers p e r ta in in g to th e u se o f firearm s, and th a t h e c o n d u c te d h im se lf in su c h a m a n n er as to b e p r e ju d ic ia l to th e r e p u ta tio n and g o o d n a m e o f th e b u rea u .”
    Absence of Any Penalty
There are other cases where the killing is irregular and questionable, where the public is actively concerned, where the police officers are tried and acquitted, and where authorities do not act to penalize the officers. The consequences of the killing of C.H., a 26-year-old black man, in Winchester, Kentucky, in August of 1966, point up this fact all too clearly. C.H. was drunk and had been yelling in the street in front of his house when officers came and tried to arrest him. H. went into his house. Not long afterwards, four officers entered the house, all with drawn guns, and the consequence was the unarmed C.H.’s death. The officers were indicted for murder by the Clark County grand jury, suspended from their jobs with pay, and tried. The jury was instructed that acquittal was proper if the policemen were acting within the law or if their actions were found to be in self-defense or in defense of another. All four policemen were found innocent of murder and, after the trial, returned to duty. There was considerable concern on the part of the black community, including direct complaints to the mayor stating that they did not trust the officers, could not respect them, and feared them. Representatives of the black community felt that the entire incident had racial overtones. The mayor said he did not believe that there were racial factors in this issue. He was quoted as adding, “If we punished a policeman for actions in the line of duty when we believe these actions were necessarily taken [sic], it would depress police morale and hamper law enforcement here.”

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 321

So, some get penalized and more do not, but in most all cases the public does not know what has happened to a policeman about whom question has been raised. The public then is in no position to evaluate the functioning of the police department in disciplining their own men. Police departments throughout the country have consistently insisted that they have the right to discipline in their way, have actively opposed all forms of outside reviewing agencies, and have been successful in this opposition. Yet there is no way for the public to know how they operate, and they operate very differently in different places and at different times. In the report of a task force for the National Commission on Causes and Prevention of Violence (1969), severe criticism was made of police internal review in general: “Secrecy and lack of procedural safeguards inevitably foster suspicion about the fairness of internal review” (p. 385). The fact that the findings are seldom disclosed to the public “deprives hearings of their value in promoting community relations” (p. 385). The report continues:
A major criticism o f internal review is that it seldom produces m eaningful discipline o f persons gu ilty o f p olice m isconduct. E ven w h en an officer is disciplined, the punishm ent is o ften so light as to be a token that aggravates rather than satisfies the grievant. B y contrast, m any departm ents im pose relatively severe penalties for violations o f m inor internal regulations. T h u s tardiness or insubordination m ay warrant an autom atic suspension that is m ore onerous than the sanction fo r physical abuse o f a citizen. . . . T h e inference is that internal review is m ore attuned to en forcin g organizational disciplines that redressing citizen grievances. ( p . 385)

## Coroner’s Inquest

In the light of the wide variety of police killings it seems strange that in our democracy approximately 99 percent of the killings are found to be noncriminal. Yet the law and legal procedures have been used to achieve this result. How? What

## 322 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

happens when a police officer kills a citizen while carrying out his duty? A large number of police killings are first dealt with by a coroner’s inquest. Such inquests are an ancient custom, having the purpose of deciding the cause of death of the individual concerned. Inquests generally can be held by the local coroner at his discretion, with or without a jury. In many jurisdictions there are no particular requirements about the character of the jury and people can be picked from the street to make a jury if they are citizens of the area. Coroners’ offices will usually hold an inquest if so requested by a district attorney, sheriff, chief of police, or city prosecutor. Rarely presided over by a judge, an inquest is not a court proceeding. The rules of evidence do not apply. Having no legal effect, the findings of the jury neither dictate nor preclude further civil or criminal action. The proceedings are run by the coroner, or often by a deputy prosecutor from the prosecutor’s office loaned to the coroner for that purpose. There are no mandatory adversary proceedings. If legal representatives of the deceased are present, they may ask questions only at the discretion of the coroner. Thus, in many situations, attorneys for the deceased or the deceased’s family are permitted to write out questions, some of which, at the discretion of the coroner or his deputy prosecutor, are asked to witnesses. No one explains the law to the jury which is supposed to make a decision of some sort even though the decision will have no legal weight. Whatever the coroner’s decision, or the coroner’s jury’s decision, the prosecutor retains the responsibility of deciding whether there is basis for criminal charges to be brought against the killer.
    Prosecutors use coroner’s jury’s decisions. Because of the
structure of the coroner hearings in police homicide cases, a structure in which the proceedings are controlled by the coroner and/or deputy prosecutor, both closely involved with the police force, coroner’s jury’s decisions are almost consistently that the homicide is noncriminal. Prosecutors then, in announcing a decision not to bring charges against the policeman killer, state that they are leaning on the decision of the coroner’s jury. The public, even the sophisticated public, does not

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 323

know the subtleties and the facts of this matter. For example, the San Francisco Examiner (1969) opened a report of the results of the coroner’s jury’s inquest into the death of A.L. by stating “Police Officer G.R. has been officially [sic] cleared of any wrongdoing in the March 30 fatal shooting of A.L., a black 19-year-old.” And unfortunately this is not uncommon. The authorities and the public media tend to deal with the coroner’s inquest as if its decision were an official and final one. In Seattle, L.W., a black veteran of the Vietnam war, was killed by Officer J.H. on May 15, 1970. The inquest jury, by a 3 to 2 vote, found the killing to be criminal, the first inquest finding against a law enforcement officer in the county in at least 20 years. The county prosecutor stated that “the verdicts of inquest juries are not binding [on him], even though all prosecuting attorneys in King County for the past 35 years have adhered to the verdicts returned by coroners’ juries” (The Seattle Times, 1970b). He added that the “split verdict” must be treated as no verdict and is analogous to a hung jury. No charges were brought against Officer J.H.
    Some students of the inquest system feel that it has endured
for two reasons. One is that in many states prosecutors do not have investigative staff and the inquest allows the prosecutor an opportunity to obtain information on which to base his decision whether to file a criminal charge. This may be especially true when a police officer is involved in a death because ordinarily the prosecutor must rely upon information supplied by the police. Yet it is very clear that much pertinent information, even vital information, may not be brought out in the inquest. The system fails on that purpose.
    The other reason why the system has endured is that the
verdict offers the prosecutor an institutional escape route for justifying a refusal not to prosecute. The figures in Seattle, not unlike the statistics in many other cities in the country, point up the issue: For at least 20 years no coroner’s jury had declared a police killing to have been criminal; for 35 years no prosecutor had made a judgment different from that of the coroner’s jury. The L.W. case changed both those figures; the coroner’s jury declared that the police killing was criminal and

## 324 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

the prosecutor refused to go along with the coroner’s jury’s decision. Coroners’ juries may have their purpose and may well have an important part in a state’s legal structure. But in the evaluation of cases of police killing civilians in the United States, it does seem clear that the coroner’s jury is a poor vehicle for discovering truth or for achieving justice.

## Prosecution in Police Homicides

There are other procedures, a grand jury or a criminal trial, but all fall short of our ideals of justice, and almost always the result is predictable—a police homicide is noncriminal. One major source of this near unanimity is the crucial role of the prosecutor. In many instances he alone makes the decision whether to bring charges against the officer or to clear him. Whatever the conclusion of a coroner’s jury—in which he or one of his deputies often plays a central part—the decision to charge still rests with him. The prosecutor leads and guides the grand jury and is the one attorney present at its secret hearings. Finally, if there are criminal charges, it is he who must prosecute the policeman. The prosecutor has a central and highly responsible position in the law enforcement structure. But in cases having to do with the police, his position is confounded by his deep and mutually dependent involvement with fellow law enforcement officers. In most of his work the prosecutor is dependent upon the police for information about and investigation of the crime, and policemen are very often the principal witnesses in criminal cases brought by the prosecutor. The relationship is crucially interdependent, and the complexity of the mutual need is emphasized in states where the prosecutor’s department does not have independent facilities for investigation. The police then are in fact an arm of the prosecutor’s office; all are an intimate part of the law enforcement apparatus of the state. In such a setting, it is almost impossible for the prosecutor to be free and unbiased.

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 325

Paul Chevigny (1969), who studied police abuses in New York City, saw the prosecutor as a central figure:
T h e p ow er o f any district attorney to p rotect the p olice and perpetuate abuses is nearly as great as his p ow er to elim inate them , and unfortunately m ost prosecutors in the U n ited States have chosen to side uncritically w ith the police, (p. 250)
The interdependence that exists between prosecutor and police runs as well through other facets of the law enforcement system. Judges too are dependent on the prosecutor, work closely with him, and in criminal cases are dependent upon police witnesses. Judges have the opportunity to be more independent than the prosecutor; they certainly are not dependent upon the police and prosecutor in the same way that the prosecutor is on the police. But they are part of the same force, whose function is to bring law, order, and justice to the community. Chevigny’s (1969) judgment about the rule of courts is pertinent here:
T h e courts do play an im portant role in protectin g the police from criticism . It is the job o f the trial courts to find facts upon the cover charges brought b y the police. T h e y have the p ow er to shield police officers from effective com plaints b y citizens, and th ey use it all to o often, (p. 251)
Chevigny notes that in ordinary assault cases a judge will consider a defense saying it was the complainant not the defendant who committed the assault. When a police officer is the complainant, however, such a defense is rarely listened to. Judges see such a defense as “an attack upon the authorities,” and “an effort to impeach a witness, the policeman, upon whose credibility he is compelled to rely in thousands of cases.” Chevigny concludes:
If the judges seem doubtful about defenses based upon p olice abuses, the reader can im agine their reaction to an attem pt to

## 326 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

prosecute a p olice officer. P olice officers prosecute citizens for assault as a m atter o f course, but I have y e t to see a citizen take ou t a sum m ons against a p olice officer for assault and g e t his case to trial, (p . 251)
Sometimes cases against police officers for killing do get to trial. Some are formalities. One city which had a policy of bringing policemen automatically to court is Denver. Bert M. Keating,1Denver district attorney, stated that he makes the decision as to whether the case is one of justifiable homicide. If so, it is filed as murder:
. . . and then m y office accepts the first 12 jurors subject to voir dire exam ination b y defense counsel. A t the con clu sion o f the state’s case I join in defense cou n sel’s m otion for directed verdict o f n o t guilty. . . . I have never had a judge, in the 17 years I have been district attorney, refuse to grant the m otion.
Mr. Keating went on to say that the importance of this sort of hearing stems in part from the fact that the statute of limitations against murder does not run in the state of Colorado, “therefore, it becomes important to have a judicial hearing as soon as possible to prevent someone, at a later time when the record is cold and witnesses have disappeared, to demand a hearing.” There are other jurisdictions with similar practices. On December 11, 1969, J.H. was killed by Patrolman P.A. in Shawnee, Oklahoma. He had been suspected of armed robbery, was trying to elude a police dragnet, and was finally cornered in a bus station. He fought with officers and was shot by P.A. when he tried to get one of the officer’s guns. The Shawnee News Star describes the procedure as follows:
T h e D A ’s office, having review ed the evidence and interview ed all w itnesses personally, finds the case to be clearly one o f justifiable hom icide. H o w e v e r , accord in g to the general custom and tradition in cases o f this typ e, the officer in volved in a hom icide under such circum stances is offered an op p ortu n ity to request

1. Keating, B. M. Personal Communication, 1965.

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 327

that charges be filed against him in order that there be a judicial determ ination o f record m ade for p osterity—since, in such cases, the statute o f lim itations runs forever. (1970)
The district attorney added that Officer P.A. had requested that charges be filed against him and the case was set for a nonjury trial. Judges then often join prosecutors in these issues. Attorney Keating’s decisions in Denver may have all been fine but it is striking that in no case did the judge differ with him on the question of probable cause to bring charges against the policeman. In some cases, judges are more explicit. In 1967 P.J., 20 years old, was killed by Sgt. R.H. in Tulsa, Oklahoma. The policeman’s partner had stopped a car after receiving information that two men in the car were trying to sell stolen suits. P.J. had no identification papers but when searched a match box was found. Officer R.H. later testified that he had attended federal narcotics seminars and had seen illegal drugs many times during his seven years on the force. Suspecting that the material in the box was marijuana, R.H. reported: “I said ‘marijuana, huh?’ and he said ‘Yeah.’ ” P.J. fled after being placed under arrest and was killed by R.H. The defense attorney asked for a directed verdict of acquittal, arguing that P.J. was a felon being in possession of marijuana, and that the killing was therefore justifiable. The judge, hearing no opposition to the request from the prosecuting attorney, noted that state law specifically says that a commissioned officer is allowed to shoot in an attempt to apprehend a felon, and that persons who are fleeing from arrest on serious charges must be stopped. He added, “Had the officer permitted the felon to escape he would have been subject to the censure of the public and his superior officers.” A more extreme statement by a judge occurred in the 1969 case of H.W., 32 years old, who was beaten in Clarksdale, Mississippi, by Police Captain L.C. and died the following day. The captain and an off-duty sergeant had gone to a cafe on a duty call; they saw H.W. who seemed drunk and asked him to walk outside. He staggered and was arrested for public

## 328 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

d ru n k en n ess. H e p r o te ste d , w a s p r o fa n e , c o n tin u e d his p ro - fa n ity th r o u g h th e b o o k in g p r o c e d u r e , and th e n b e g a n to resist p h y s ic a lly , lu n g in g at L .C . w h o a d m itted to h ittin g him w it h his b la c k ja c k . H .W . c o u ld n ’t b e a w a k e n e d th e fo llo w in g m o r n in g and w a s assu m ed to b e still drunk. O n th a t a fte r n o o n a d o c to r w a s ca lled ; H .W . w a s ta k en to th e h o sp ita l w ith a fr a c tu r e d sk u ll an d d ied th a t n ig h t a fte r h a v in g brain su rg ery . T h e ca p ta in w a s c h a r g e d w it h m a n sla u g h ter. T h e d efen se a tto r n e y said th a t L .C . h ad d o n e w h a t th e p u b lic p a y s h im fo r and w h a t h e w a s r eq u ired to d o b y la w in d ea lin g w ith “this m e n a c e to th e c o m m u n ity at la r g e .’- T h e c o u n t y ju d g e dism issed th e c h a r g e o n th e g r o u n d s th a t th e officer had “a p erf e c t r ig h t to d e fe n d h im se lf,” ad d in g:
I see noth in g w ro n g w ith w hat Captain C. has done. I alm ost get sick at m y stom ach w h en people start y ellin g “police brutality.” T h e y w o n ’t g et anyw here in m y court this w ay.

## Trials

There are, however, real trials, where true adversary proceedings take place before an impartial judge and a jury. In studying police homicides I made a special effort to follow up on cases where criminal trials were held, but could only discover three that concluded with criminal penalties. One case, reportedly the first successful prosecution of a law enforcement officer for murder in the state of California, concerned Los Angeles Deputy Sheriff R.S., 25 years old and white, who killed R.M., a 20-year-old black man on August 18, 1969. Four deputies went to a gift shop in response to a “burglary in progress” call, saw a front window broken; three entered the store, one going to guard the back door. R.S. saw M. hiding in the rear of the shop, later testified that he believed that M. attempted to shoot him. The policeman fired one shot, hitting M. in the abdomen and killing him. A 22caliber revolver was found at the scene by homicide detectives. A coroner’s inquest returned a unanimous verdict of “death

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 329

not by criminal means,” the California method of saying that it was a justifiable killing. However, two months later Deputy Sheriff R.S. was dismissed from his post and booked on the charge of suspicion of murder in the killing. A careful statement by Los Angeles County Sheriff Pitchess included the following:
T h e initial investigation b y sheriff’s hom icide detectives revealed that M. reportedly was in possession o f a 22-caliber, 2-inch revolver. U p o n recen tly receivin g n ew evidence, I im m ediately instructed hom icide d etectives to reopen the case and as a result o f hundreds o f hours o f additional investigation, inform ation and evidence w ere developed that resulted in the dismissal o f S. from the sheriff’s departm ent and im m ediate arrest fo r murder.
The new evidence was not to be released for fear of jeopardizing the case, which Pitchess called a very difficult one. The sheriff would only say that the probe was continued “in spite of the coroner’s inquest.” After a closed preliminary hearing, R.S. was bound over for trial in Los Angeles Superior Court on a murder charge. Two detectives from the sheriff’s homicide division explained:
D irectly after the incident took place, w e w ere inform ed that perhaps R .S.’s story as he told it to the inquest jury was n ot true; that perhaps M. did n ot have a gun in the darkened g ift shop. T h ere w ere several allegations that M . w as totally unarm ed. A n investigation revealed that M. had no gun at the scene o f the crim e and that S. had lied to the coron er’s inquest. A t this p oin t it was also obvious that S. had falsified his official report.
Eventually R.S. was found guilty of second degree murder and sentenced to 5-years-to-life in state prison for the fatal shooting of M. During the trial the prosecution contended that S. had planted a gun near M. after shooting him in the stomach. The chief witness against the policeman at the trial was his partner at the time of the shooting. The partner testified that the planted gun was passed around after the shooting to avoid possible negative results on M’s fingerprints. Superior

## 330 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

Court Judge James G. Kolts, on imposing sentence, said: “This court cannot condone unlawful violence perpetrated by those who have the sanction of society to use force to protect and maintain society.” A second case, also a “first instance of an alleged murder-inuniform in city history,” occurred in Portland, Oregon, in
 1969. Patrolman S.S. was charged with first degree murder,
accused of deliberately shooting C.H., a 27-year-old fugitive, from a distance of four feet with a 12-gauge shotgun while H. was crouched on his knees and elbows in a bush where he hid while trying to elude the police. The shotgun S.S. used was an unauthorized weapon. S.S. had deliberately travelled some seven miles out of his district to serve the felony warrant on C.H., and it was alleged at the trial that S.S. was having a sexual affair with the wife of C.H. The jury was instructed it could return one of four verdicts: first degree murder, second degree murder, manslaughter, or acquittal. They found S.S. guilty of manslaughter. He was sentenced to five years on that charge and three years for perjury, to be served concurrently. The third case which resulted in a criminal penalty concerned the death in 1967 in Memphis, Tennessee, of M.S., 48 years old, who died a few hours after being taken to the Memphis. City Jail following an arrest for drunkenness. A few days later, a city jail “turnkey” was charged with murder by the police. The Shelby County grand jury indicted L.B., a 32 year old black, a policeman for four years, on a first degree murder charge in the death of M.S. The trial was marked by charges of racism and frame-up and the policeman denied all accusations of having mistreated his prisoner. The jury finally found L.B. guilty of involuntary manslaughter and sentenced him to one day in jail. These are rare results of not so strange cases; the patterns of fact are repeated time and time again. There is considerable circumstantial evidence that planting of weapons by the police on their victims is not as unusual as public information suggests (Berkley, 1969). For example, a grand jury report on the killings of two brothers, G.P. and R.P., in Groton, Connecti-

## UNNECESSARY POLICE FORCE / 331

cut, in 1969 stated that the four involved Connecticut state troopers lied to the coroner when they denied an informer was involved, and when they stated one of the brothers had opened fire first. The facts, according to the grand jury, were that the brothers were unarmed and, after they were killed, one of the troopers panicked. “All I could think of is what the papers are going to say and what it was going to do to the state police. . . . They’ll kill us, they’ll crucify us, these guys weren’t armed.” So Sergeant R.G. took out his own .45, fired a shot in the wall from where the bodies were, and dropped— planted—the gun on the floor. . . .
There is far too intimate an involvement among police, prosecutor, mayor, and judge, among all members of the law enforcement establishment. The fact that justice is not available, that there is form without substance, that there is talk without meaning gives rise to bitterness, a bitterness which is deserved and understandable, on the part of members of minority groups who are often the victims of police killings. They are urged to respect law and to expect order and justice, but they can see, when they are directly involved, that justice is not available. They see hypocrisy. This often leads to a persistent and generalized suspiciousness of the legal system and of those in power. The suspiciousness and skepticism is reaffirmed every time a policeman is involved in an action against a citizen, particularly when the act is something society says it holds to be heinous, namely, the killing of an individual. Our leaders talk about the need to limit violence, yet in cases where the violence is done by representatives of the state it appears to be condoned. Clearly, if this substantive problem of police killing is not dealt with in more just fashion, it will continue to exacerbate the already aggravated problems between the police and the poor communities in all cities. Human relations efforts alone cannot bring about the trust and respect of law, order, and justice crucial to a democratic society.

## 332 / POLICE MISCONDUCT

## About the Editor

## R IC H A RD J . L U N D M A N

Richard J. Lundman, Associate Professor of Sociology at The Ohio State University, received his B.A. from Beloit College (1966), M.A. from the University of Illinois (1968), and Ph.D. from the University of Minnesota (1972). Prior to affiliation with Ohio State, he taught at the University of Delaware. His teaching and research interests include police and policing, deviance, criminology, and organizational deviance. He has presented papers at numerous national meetings, authored chapters for The Police in Society, Police Roles in the Seventies: Volume II, and Critical Issues in Criminal Justice, co-authored Corporate and Governmental Deviance (Oxford University Press, 1978) and Organizational Deviance (Holt, Rinehart and Winston, 1981), authored Police and Policing (Holt, Rinehart and Winston, 1980), and contributed articles to Journal of Police Science and Administration, Criminology, Social Problems, Crime and Delinquency, The Sociological Quarterly, The Journal of Research in Crime and Delinquency, The Journal of Criminal Law and Criminology, and Law and Policy Quarterly. He also has taught at the New Castle County Police Academy and now teaches a section on unnecessary police force at the Columbus Police Academy.
